scope="row">Fuel</th><td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LH2" title="LH2">LH2</a>/<a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">LOX</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Saturn V</b> (pronounced "Saturn five") was an American <a href="/wiki/Human-rating_certification" title="Human-rating certification">human-rated</a> <a href="/wiki/Expendable_launch_system" title="Expendable launch system">expendable</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> used by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> between 1967 and 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Multistage_rocket" title="Multistage rocket">three-stage</a> <a href="/wiki/Liquid-propellant_rocket" title="Liquid-propellant rocket">liquid-propellant</a> <a href="/wiki/Super_heavy-lift_launch_vehicle" title="Super heavy-lift launch vehicle">super heavy-lift launch vehicle</a> was developed to support the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> for human <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Moon" title="Exploration of the Moon">exploration of the Moon</a> and was later used to launch <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a>, the first American space station.\n</p><p>The Saturn V was launched 13 times from the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> with no loss of crew or payload. As of  2018,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the Saturn V remains the tallest, heaviest, and most powerful (highest <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">total impulse</a>) rocket ever brought to operational status, and holds records for the heaviest payload launched and largest payload capacity to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO) of 140,000&#160;kg (310,000&#160;lb), which included the third stage and unburned propellant needed to send the Apollo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">Command/Service Module</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-Launch_Capabilities_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-Launch_Capabilities-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Report_of_the_Synthesis_Group_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Report_of_the_Synthesis_Group-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest production model of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">Saturn family</a> of rockets, the Saturn V was designed under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rudolph" title="Arthur Rudolph">Arthur Rudolph</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft_Company" title="Douglas Aircraft Company">Douglas Aircraft Company</a>, and <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> as the lead contractors.\n</p><p>To date<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Saturn V remains the only launch vehicle to carry humans beyond low Earth orbit. A total of 15 flight-capable vehicles were built, but only 13 were flown. An additional three vehicles were built for ground testing purposes. A <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">total of 24 astronauts</a> were launched to the Moon, three of them twice, in the four years spanning December 1968 through December 1972.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Historical_background"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Historical background</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Saturn_development"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Saturn development</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-3"><a href="#C-1_to_C-4"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">C-1 to C-4</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-4"><a href="#C-5"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">C-5</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Selection_for_Apollo_lunar_landing"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Selection for Apollo lunar landing</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Technology"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Technology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Stages"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Stages</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-8"><a Unit</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Range_safety"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Range safety</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Comparisons"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Comparisons</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Titan_II"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Titan II</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Soviet_N1-L3"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Soviet N1-L3</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#U.S._Space_Shuttle"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">U.S. Space Shuttle</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Soviet_Energia/Buran"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span vehicles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Space_Launch_System"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Space Launch System</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#S-IC_thrust_comparisons"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">S-IC thrust comparisons</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Assembly"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Assembly</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Lunar_mission_launch_sequence"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Lunar mission launch sequence</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#S-IC_sequence"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">S-IC sequence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#S-II_sequence"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">S-II sequence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#S-IVB_sequence"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">S-IVB sequence</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Skylab"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Skylab</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Proposed_post-Apollo_developments"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Proposed post-Apollo developments</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Cost"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Cost</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Saturn_V_vehicles_and_launches"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Saturn V vehicles and launches</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Proposed_successors"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Proposed successors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Saturn_V_displays"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Saturn V displays</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Saturn_V_in_fiction"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Saturn V in fiction</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#NASA_sites"><span class="tocnumber">17.1</span> <span class="toctext">NASA sites</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Other_sites"><span class="tocnumber">17.2</span> <span class="toctext">Other sites</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Simulators"><span class="tocnumber">17.3</span> <span class="toctext">Simulators</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_background">Historical background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historical background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a></div>\n<p>The origins of the Saturn V rocket begin with the US government bringing <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> along with about seven hundred German rocket engineers and technicians to the United States in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Paperclip" title="Operation Paperclip">Operation Paperclip</a>, a program authorized by President Truman in August 1946 with the purpose of harvesting Germany\'s rocket expertise, to give the US an edge in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> through development of <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-range_ballistic_missile" title="Intermediate-range ballistic missile">intermediate-range</a> (IRBM) and <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">intercontinental ballistic missiles</a> (ICBM). It was known that America\'s rival, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, would also try to secure some of the Germans.\n</p><p>Von Braun was put into the rocket design division of the Army due to his prior direct involvement in the creation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V-2" title="V-2">V-2</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE-12">[8]</a></sup> Between 1945 and 1958, his work was restricted to conveying the ideas and methods behind the V-2 to the American engineers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite Von Braun\'s many articles on the future of space rocketry, the US Government continued funding Air Force and Navy rocket programs to test their <a href="/wiki/Project_Vanguard" title="Project Vanguard">Vanguard</a> missiles in spite of numerous costly failures.\n</p><p>It was not until the 1957 Soviet launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik</a> 1 atop an <a href="/wiki/R-7_(rocket_family)" title="R-7 (rocket family)">R-7</a> <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">ICBM</a>, capable of carrying a <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">thermonuclear warhead</a> to the US,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[10]</a></sup> that the Army and the government started taking serious steps towards putting Americans in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup> Finally, they turned to von Braun and his team, who during these years created and experimented with the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter-C" title="Jupiter-C">Jupiter series of rockets</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Juno_I" title="Juno I">Juno I</a> was the rocket that launched the <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">first American satellite</a> in January 1958, and part of the last-ditch plan for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NACA" title="NACA">NACA</a> (the predecessor of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>) to get its foot in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-Williams_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-Williams-16">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jupiter-C" title="Jupiter-C">Jupiter</a> series was one more step in von Braun\'s journey to the Saturn V, later calling that first series "an infant Saturn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_development">Saturn development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Saturn development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">Saturn (rocket family)</a></div>\n<p>The Saturn V\'s design stemmed from the designs of the Jupiter series rockets. As the success of the Jupiter series became evident, the Saturn series emerged.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="C-1_to_C-4">C-1 to C-4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: C-1 to C-4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Between 1960 and 1962, the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> (MSFC) designed a series of Saturn rockets that could be used for various Earth orbit or lunar missions.\n</p><p>The C-1 was developed into the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">Saturn I</a>, and the C-2 rocket was dropped early in the design process in favor of the C-3, which was intended to use two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1</a> engines on its first stage, four <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">J-2</a> engines for its second stage, and an S-IV stage, using six <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10</a> engines.\n</p><p>NASA planned to use the C-3 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit_rendezvous" title="Earth orbit rendezvous">Earth Orbit Rendezvous</a> (EOR) concept, with at least four or five launches needed for a single lunar mission.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> But MSFC was already planning an even bigger rocket, the C-4, which would use four F-1 engines on its first stage, an enlarged C-3 second stage, and the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a>, a stage with a single J-2 engine, as its third stage. The C-4 would need only two launches to carry out an EOR lunar mission.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="C-5">C-5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: C-5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On January 10, 1962, NASA announced plans to build the C-5. The three-stage rocket would consist of: the S-IC first stage, with five <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">F-1</a> engines; the S-II second stage, with five J-2 engines; and the S-IVB third stage, with a single J-2 engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup> The C-5 was designed for a 90,000-pound (41,000&#160;kg) payload capacity to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The C-5 would undergo component testing even before the first model was constructed. The S-IVB third stage would be used as the second stage for the C-IB, which would serve both to demonstrate proof of concept and feasibility for the C-5, but would also provide flight data critical to development of the C-5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup> Rather than undergoing testing for each major component, the C-5 would be tested in an "all-up" fashion, meaning that the first test flight of the rocket would include complete versions of all three stages. By testing all components at once, far fewer test flights would be required before a manned launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-expeditions3.4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-expeditions3.4-18">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The C-5 was confirmed as NASA\'s choice for the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> in early 1963, and was named the Saturn&#160;V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup> The C-1 became the Saturn I, and C-1B became Saturn IB. Von Braun headed a team at the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in building a vehicle capable of launching a manned spacecraft on a trajectory to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before they moved under NASA\'s jurisdiction, von Braun\'s team had already begun work on improving the thrust, creating a less complex operating system, and designing better mechanical systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup> It was during these revisions that the decision to reject the single engine of the V-2\'s design came about, and the team moved to a multiple-engine design. The Saturn I and IB reflected these changes, but were not large enough to send a manned spacecraft to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup> These designs, however, provided a basis for which NASA could determine its best method towards landing a man on the Moon.\n</p><p>The Saturn V\'s final design had several key features. Engineers determined that the best engines were the F-1s coupled with the new liquid hydrogen propulsion system called J-2, which made the Saturn C-5 configuration optimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup> By 1962, NASA had finalized its plans to proceed with von Braun\'s Saturn designs, and the Apollo space program gained speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing-Saturn_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing-Saturn-19">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the configuration finalized, NASA turned its attention to mission profiles. Despite some controversy, a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_rendezvous" title="Lunar orbit rendezvous">lunar orbit rendezvous</a> for the lunar module was chosen over an Earth orbital rendezvous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bilstein_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bilstein-15">[11]</a></sup> Issues such as type of fuel injections, the needed amount of fuel for such a trip, and rocket manufacturing processes were ironed out, and the designs for the Saturn V were selected. The stages were designed by von Braun\'s <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in Huntsville, and outside contractors were chosen for the construction: <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> (<a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">S-IC</a>), <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> (<a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a>), <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft_Company" title="Douglas Aircraft Company">Douglas Aircraft</a> (<a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a>), and <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Instrument Unit</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing-Saturn_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing-Saturn-19">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection_for_Apollo_lunar_landing">Selection for Apollo lunar landing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Selection for Apollo lunar landing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Apollo#Choosing_a_mission_mode" title="Project Apollo">Project Apollo &#167;&#160;Choosing a mission mode</a></div>\n<p>Early in the planning process, NASA considered three leading ideas for the Moon mission: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_Orbit_Rendezvous" title="Earth Orbit Rendezvous">Earth Orbit Rendezvous</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_Ascent" title="Direct Ascent">Direct Ascent</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbit_Rendezvous" title="Lunar Orbit Rendezvous">Lunar Orbit Rendezvous</a> (LOR). A direct ascent configuration would require an extremely large rocket to send a three-man spacecraft to land directly on the lunar surface. Earth orbit rendezvous would launch the direct-landing spacecraft in two smaller parts which would combine in Earth orbit. A LOR mission would involve a single rocket launching two spacecraft: a mother ship, and a smaller, two-man landing module which would rendezvous back with the main spacecraft in lunar orbit. The lander would be discarded and the mother ship would return home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-expeditions3.2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-expeditions3.2-20">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA at first dismissed LOR as a riskier option, given that a <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">space rendezvous</a> had yet to be performed in Earth orbit, much less in lunar orbit. Several NASA officials, including Langley Research Center engineer <a href="/wiki/John_Houbolt" title="John Houbolt">John Houbolt</a> and NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/George_Low" title="George Low">George Low</a>, argued that a Lunar Orbit Rendezvous provided the simplest landing on the Moon with the most cost&#8211;efficient launch vehicle, and the best chance to accomplish the lunar landing within the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup> Other NASA officials were convinced, and LOR was officially selected as the mission configuration for the Apollo program on November 7, 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stages1_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-stages1-17">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_v_schematic.jpg"><img alt="Drawing of a Saturn V rocket, showing all the stages of the rocket with brief descriptions and two tiny people to show relative size." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1143" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Saturn_v_schematic.jpg/220px-Saturn_v_schematic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Saturn_v_schematic.jpg/330px-Saturn_v_schematic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Saturn_v_schematic.jpg/440px-Saturn_v_schematic.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_v_schematic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saturn V diagram</div></div></div>\n<p>The Saturn V\'s size and payload capacity dwarfed all other previous rockets which had successfully flown at that time. With the Apollo spacecraft on top, it stood 363 feet (111&#160;m) tall, and without fins, it was 33 feet (10&#160;m) in diameter. Fully fueled, the Saturn V weighed 6.5 million&#160;pounds (2,950,000&#160;kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svgiw_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-svgiw-4">[4]</a></sup> and had a <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> payload capacity originally estimated at 261,000 pounds (118,000&#160;kg),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> but was designed to send at least 90,000 pounds (41,000&#160;kg) to the Moon.\n</p><p>Later upgrades increased that capacity; during the final three Apollo lunar missions it deployed about 310,000 pounds (140,000&#160;kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-Launch_Capabilities_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Space-Launch_Capabilities-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Report_of_the_Synthesis_Group_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Report_of_the_Synthesis_Group-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infoNoteLEO_group=note_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-infoNoteLEO_group=note-7">[note 1]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">LEO</a> and sent up to 107,100&#160;lb (48,600&#160;kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svgiw_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-svgiw-4">[4]</a></sup> spacecraft to the Moon. At a height of 363 feet (111&#160;m), the Saturn V was 58 feet (18&#160;m) taller than the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a> from the ground to the torch, and 48 feet (15&#160;m) taller than the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ben" title="Big Ben">Big Ben</a> clock tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bong_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bong-22">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_Launch_Vehicle" title="Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle">Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle</a> used on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_7" title="Freedom 7">Freedom 7</a></i>, the first manned American spaceflight, was just under 11 feet (3.4&#160;m) longer than the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> stage, and delivered less sea level thrust (78,000 pounds-force (350&#160;kN)) than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft#Launch_Escape_System_(LES)" title="Apollo spacecraft">Launch Escape System</a> rocket (150,000 pounds-force (667&#160;kN) sea level thrust) mounted atop the Apollo Command Module.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Saturn V was principally designed by the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>, although numerous major systems, including propulsion, were designed by subcontractors. It used the powerful new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J-2_(rocket_engine)" title="J-2 (rocket engine)">J-2</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket engines</a> for propulsion. When tested at Stennis Space Center, these engines shattered the windows of nearby houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stennis_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stennis-24">[20]</a></sup> Designers decided early on to attempt to use as much technology from the Saturn I program as possible. Consequently, the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a>-500 third stage of the Saturn V was based on the S-IVB-200 second stage of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">Saturn IB</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Instrument Unit</a> that controlled the Saturn V shared characteristics with that carried by the Saturn IB.\n</p><p>Blueprints and other Saturn V plans are available on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microfilm" title="Microfilm">microfilm</a> at the Marshall Space Flight Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSFC-plans_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSFC-plans-25">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages">Stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Saturn V consisted of three stages&#8212;the S-IC first stage, S-II second stage and the S-IVB third stage&#8212;and the instrument unit. All three stages used <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> (LOX) as the <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizer</a>. The first stage used <a href="/wiki/RP-1" title="RP-1">RP-1</a> for fuel, while the second and third stages used <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> (LH2). The upper stages also used small solid-propellant <a href="/wiki/Ullage_motor" title="Ullage motor">ullage motors</a> that helped to separate the stages during the launch, and to ensure that the liquid propellants were in a proper position to be drawn into the pumps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="S-IC_first_stage">S-IC first stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: S-IC first stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">S-IC</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1645" data-file-width="1282" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg/220px-Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg/330px-Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg/440px-Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_8_first_stage_in_the_Vehicle_Assembly_Building.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first stage of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> Saturn V being erected in the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">VAB</a> on February 1, 1968</div></div></div>\n<p>The S-IC was built by the <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> Company at the <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_Tank" title="External Tank">External Tanks</a> would later be built by <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a>. Most of its mass at launch was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipropellant_rocket" title="Bipropellant rocket">propellant</a>, <a href="/wiki/RP-1" title="RP-1">RP-1</a> fuel with <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-launchvehicles_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-launchvehicles-27">[23]</a></sup> It was 138 feet (42&#160;m) tall and 33 feet (10&#160;m) in diameter, and provided over 7,600,000 pounds-force (34,000&#160;kN) of thrust. The S-IC stage had a dry weight of about 289,000 pounds (131 metric tons) and fully fueled at launch had a total weight of 5,100,000 pounds (2,300 metric tons). It was powered by five <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">Rocketdyne F-1</a> engines arrayed in a <a href="/wiki/Quincunx" title="Quincunx">quincunx</a> (five units, with four arranged in a square, and the fifth in the center) The center engine was held in a fixed position, while the four outer engines could be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulically</a> turned <a href="/wiki/Gimbal" title="Gimbal">(gimballed)</a> to steer the rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-launchvehicles_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-launchvehicles-27">[23]</a></sup> In flight, the center engine was turned off about 26 seconds earlier than the outboard engines to limit acceleration. During launch, the S-IC fired its engines for 168 seconds (ignition occurred about 8.9 seconds before liftoff) and at engine cutoff, the vehicle was at an altitude of about 36 nautical miles (67&#160;km), was downrange about 50 nautical miles (93&#160;km), and was moving about 7,500 feet per second (2,300&#160;m/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlightManual503&#167;&#160;4_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FlightManual503&#167;_4-28">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="S-II_second_stage">S-II second stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: S-II second stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V,_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg/220px-Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg/330px-Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg/440px-Saturn_V%2C_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V,_S-II_Stage_is_Lifted_into_Test_Stand_-_GPN-2000-000540.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An S-II stage hoisted onto the A-2 test stand at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_Test_Facility" title="Mississippi Test Facility">Mississippi Test Facility</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The S-II was built by <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> at <a href="/wiki/Seal_Beach,_California" title="Seal Beach, California">Seal Beach, California</a>. Using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LH2" title="LH2">liquid hydrogen</a> and liquid oxygen, it had five <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">Rocketdyne J-2</a> engines in a similar arrangement to the S-IC, also using the outer engines for control. The S-II was 81.6 feet (24.87&#160;m) tall with a diameter of 33 feet (10&#160;m), identical to the S-IC, and thus was the largest cryogenic stage until the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> in 1981. The S-II had a dry weight of about 80,000 pounds (36,000&#160;kg) and fully fueled, weighed 1,060,000 pounds (480,000&#160;kg). The second stage accelerated the Saturn V through the upper atmosphere with 1,100,000 pounds-force (4,900&#160;kN) of thrust in vacuum.\n</p><p>When loaded, significantly more than 90 percent of the mass of the stage was propellant; however, the ultra-lightweight design had led to two failures in structural testing. Instead of having an intertank structure to separate the two fuel tanks as was done in the S-IC, the S-II used a common bulkhead that was constructed from both the top of the LOX tank and bottom of the LH2 tank. It consisted of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a> sheets separated by a honeycomb structure made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenolic_resin" title="Phenolic resin">phenolic resin</a>. This bulkhead had to insulate against the 126&#160;&#176;F (70&#160;&#176;C) temperature difference between the two tanks. The use of a common bulkhead saved 7,900 pounds (3.6&#160;t). Like the S-IC, the S-II was transported from its manufacturing plant to the Cape by sea.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="S-IVB_third_stage">S-IVB third stage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: S-IVB third stage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg/220px-SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg/330px-SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg/440px-SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SaturnV_S-IVB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cutaway drawing of the Saturn V S-IVB</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> was built by the <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft_Company" title="Douglas Aircraft Company">Douglas Aircraft Company</a> at <a href="/wiki/Huntington_Beach,_California" title="Huntington Beach, California">Huntington Beach, California</a>. It had one J-2 engine and used the same fuel as the S-II. The S-IVB used a common bulkhead to separate the two tanks. It was 58.6 feet (17.86&#160;m) tall with a diameter of 21.7 feet (6.604&#160;m) and was also designed with high mass efficiency, though not quite as aggressively as the S-II. The S-IVB had a dry weight of about 23,000 pounds (10,000&#160;kg) and, fully fueled, weighed about 262,000 pounds (119,000&#160;kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_S-IVB_Stage_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_S-IVB_Stage-29">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The S-IVB-500 model used on the Saturn V differed from the S-IVB-200 used as the second stage of the Saturn IB, in that the engine was restartable once per mission. This was necessary as the stage would be used twice during a lunar mission: first in a 2.5 min burn for the orbit insertion after second stage cutoff, and later for the <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">trans-lunar injection</a> (TLI) burn, lasting about 6 min. Two liquid-fueled Auxiliary Propulsion System (APS) units mounted at the aft end of the stage were used for attitude control during the <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a> and the trans-lunar phases of the mission. The two APSs were also used as <a href="/wiki/Ullage_motor" title="Ullage motor">ullage engines</a> to settle the propellants in the aft tank engine feed lines prior to the trans-lunar injection burn.\n</p><p>The S-IVB was the only rocket stage of the Saturn V small enough to be transported by plane, in this case the <a href="/wiki/Aero_Spacelines_Pregnant_Guppy" title="Aero Spacelines Pregnant Guppy">Pregnant Guppy</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Instrument_Unit">Instrument Unit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Instrument Unit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Saturn V Instrument Unit</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IU-501_From_Below.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2377" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/IU-501_From_Below.jpg/220px-IU-501_From_Below.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/IU-501_From_Below.jpg/330px-IU-501_From_Below.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/IU-501_From_Below.jpg/440px-IU-501_From_Below.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IU-501_From_Below.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Instrument Unit</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_4" title="Apollo 4">Apollo 4</a> Saturn V</div></div></div>\n<p>The Instrument Unit was built by <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and rode atop the third stage. It was constructed at the Space Systems Center in Huntsville, Alabama. This computer controlled the operations of the rocket from just before liftoff until the S-IVB was discarded. It included guidance and <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> systems for the rocket. By measuring the acceleration and vehicle <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude</a>, it could calculate the position and velocity of the rocket and correct for any deviations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_safety">Range safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Range safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the event of an abort requiring the destruction of the rocket, the range safety officer would remotely shut down the engines and after several seconds send another command for the shaped explosive charges attached to the outer surfaces of the rocket to detonate. These would make cuts in fuel and oxidizer tanks to disperse the fuel quickly and to minimize mixing. The pause between these actions would give time for the crew to escape using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft#Launch_Escape_System_(LES)" title="Apollo spacecraft">Launch Escape Tower</a> or (in the later stages of the flight) the propulsion system of the Service module. A third command, "safe", was used after the S-IVB stage reached orbit to irreversibly deactivate the self-destruct system. The system was also inactive as long as the rocket was still on the launch pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSFC-RSO_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSFC-RSO-30">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparisons">Comparisons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Comparisons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titan_II">Titan II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Titan II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Saturn V had a much lower thrust-to-weight ratio than <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Titan_II_GLV" title="Titan II GLV">Titan II GLV</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_F._Gordon,_Jr." title="Richard F. Gordon, Jr.">Richard F. Gordon, Jr.</a> described Saturn as "an old man\'s ride", with "a lot more shake-rattle-and-roll" but milder thrust. <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a> and other Apollo 11 astronauts agreed that they could not tell when Saturn liftoff occurred except from instruments, unlike on Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agle199809a_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-agle199809a-31">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_N1-L3">Soviet N1-L3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Soviet N1-L3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N1%2BSaturn5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3527" data-file-width="1909" decoding="async" height="406" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/N1%2BSaturn5.jpg/220px-N1%2BSaturn5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/N1%2BSaturn5.jpg/330px-N1%2BSaturn5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/N1%2BSaturn5.jpg/440px-N1%2BSaturn5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N1%2BSaturn5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of the U.S. Saturn V rocket with the Soviet <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">N1-L3</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet space program</a>\'s counterpart to the Saturn V was <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">N1-L3</a>. The Saturn V was taller, heavier, and had greater payload capacity, both to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translunar_injection" title="Translunar injection">translunar injection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-SV_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-SV-32">[28]</a></sup> The N-1 was a three-stage launch vehicle with more liftoff thrust and a larger first stage diameter than the Saturn V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-N1-33">[29]</a></sup> It was to carry the 209,000&#160;lb (95,000&#160;kg) L3 vehicle into orbit.\n</p><p>The L3 contained an Earth departure stage, which would send to the Moon a 51,800&#160;lb (23,500&#160;kg) package which contained another stage for <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">lunar orbit</a> insertion and <a href="/wiki/Powered_Descent_Initiation" title="Powered Descent Initiation">powered descent initiation</a>, a single-<a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">cosmonaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Lunar_lander" title="Lunar lander">lander</a>, and a two-cosmonaut lunar orbiter for the return to Earth. The N1/L3 would have produced more total <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">impulse</a> (product of thrust and time) in its first four stages than the three-stage Saturn V, but it was not able to convert as much of this into payload <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> (product of mass and velocity).\n</p><p>The N1 never became operational; four test launches each resulted in catastrophic vehicle failure early in flight, and the program was canceled. Korolev elected to cluster 30 relatively small engines for the first stage, rather than develop a large engine like the <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">Rocketdyne F-1</a>.\n</p><p>The three-stage Saturn V grew over its lifetime to a peak thrust of at least 7,650,000&#160;lbf (34,020&#160;kN) (AS-510 and subsequent)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP4206_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP4206-34">[30]</a></sup> and a lift capacity of 310,000&#160;lb (140,000&#160;kg) to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Earth_Orbit" title="Low Earth Orbit">LEO</a>. The AS-510 mission (<a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a>) had a liftoff thrust of 7,823,000&#160;lbf (34,800&#160;kN). The AS-513 mission (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skylab_1" title="Skylab 1">Skylab 1</a>) had slightly greater liftoff thrust of 7,891,000&#160;lbf (35,100&#160;kN). By comparison, the N-1 had a sea-level liftoff thrust of about 10,200,000&#160;lbf (45,400&#160;kN).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> No other operational launch vehicle has ever surpassed the Saturn V in height, weight, total <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">impulse</a>, or payload capability. The closest contenders were the US <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> and the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Energia" title="Energia">Energia</a>.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>\n</th>\n<th>Saturn V (<a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;lbf (8.153<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;kN)&#183;s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Translunar payload</td>\n<td>100,740&#160;lb .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">8</span></sup> slug-ft/s (4.95<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span efficiency</td>\n<td>6.17%</td>\n<td>3.12%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Space_Shuttle">U.S. Space Shuttle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: U.S. Space Shuttle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> generated a peak thrust of 6,800,000&#160;lbf (30,100&#160;kN),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STS-107_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-STS-107-40">[36]</a></sup> and payload capacity to LEO (excluding the Orbiter itself) was 63,500 pounds (28,800&#160;kg), which was about 25 percent of the Saturn V\'s payload. Total mass in orbit, including the Orbiter, was about 247,000&#160;lb (112,000&#160;kg), compared to the Apollo 15 total orbital mass of the S-IVB third stage and Apollo spacecraft, of 309,771&#160;lb (140,510&#160;kg),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presskit15_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-presskit15-41">[37]</a></sup> some 62,800&#160;lb (28,500&#160;kg) heavier than the Shuttle was rated to carry to LEO.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Soviet_Energia.2FBuran"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_Energia/Buran">Soviet Energia/Buran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Soviet Energia/Buran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Energia" title="Energia">Energia</a> had a liftoff thrust of 7,826,000&#160;lbf (34,810&#160;kN),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> and flew twice in 1987 and 1988, the second time as the launcher for the <a href="/wiki/Buran_(spacecraft)" title="Buran (spacecraft)">Buran shuttle</a>. However, both the Energia and Buran programs were cancelled in 1993. Hypothetical future versions of Energia might have been significantly more powerful than the Saturn V, delivering 10,000,000&#160;lbf (46,000&#160;kN) of thrust and able to deliver up to 386,000&#160;lb (175&#160;t) to LEO in the "Vulkan" configuration. Planned uprated versions of the Saturn V using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1A</a> engines would have had about 18 percent more thrust and 302,580 pounds (137,250&#160;kg) payload.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-MLV_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-MLV-43">[39]</a></sup> NASA contemplated building larger members of the Saturn family, such as the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_C-8" title="Saturn C-8">Saturn C-8</a>, and also unrelated rockets, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nova_rocket" title="Nova rocket">Nova</a>, but these were never produced.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_vehicles">Other vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some other recent US launch vehicles have significantly lower launch capacity to LEO than Saturn V: the US <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV" title="Delta IV">Delta 4 Heavy</a> capacity is 63,470&#160;lb (28,790&#160;kg), the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> 551 has a capacity of 41,478&#160;lb (18,814&#160;kg), and the <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy" title="Falcon Heavy">Falcon Heavy</a> has a capacity of 140,700&#160;lb (63,800&#160;kg). The European <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a> ES delivers up to 46,000&#160;lb (21,000&#160;kg) and the Russian <a href="/wiki/Proton-M" title="Proton-M">Proton-M</a> can launch 49,000&#160;lb (22,000&#160;kg).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Launch_System">Space Launch System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Space Launch System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a>, planned for its first flight in 2020, in its final configuration is planned to be 400 feet (120&#160;m) tall with payload, and lift up to 290,000 pounds (130,000&#160;kg) into low Earth orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="S-IC_thrust_comparisons">S-IC thrust comparisons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: S-IC thrust comparisons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1240" data-file-width="1753" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf/page1-220px-Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf/page1-330px-Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf/page1-440px-Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo17_Ascent_Trajectory.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 17 ascent flight parameters</div></div></div>\n<p>Because of its large size, attention is often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> focused on the <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">S-IC</a> thrust and how this compares to other large rockets. However, several factors make such comparisons more complex than first appears:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Commonly referenced thrust numbers are a <i>specification</i>, not an actual measurement. Individual stages and engines may fall short or exceed the specification, sometimes significantly.</li>\n<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1</a> thrust <i>specification</i> was uprated beginning with <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a> (SA-510) from 1,500,000&#160;lbf (6,670&#160;kN) to 1,520,000&#160;lbf (6,770&#160;kN), yielding 7,610,000&#160;lbf (33,850&#160;kN) for the S-IC stage. The higher thrust was achieved via a redesign of the injector orifices and a slightly higher propellant mass flow rate. However, comparing the specified number to the actual measured thrust of 7,800,000&#160;lbf (34,800&#160;kN) on Apollo 15 shows a significant difference.</li>\n<li>There is no way to directly measure thrust of a rocket in flight; Rather, a mathematical calculation is made from combustion chamber pressure, <a href="/wiki/Turbopump" title="Turbopump">turbopump</a> speed, calculated propellant density and flow rate, nozzle design, and atmospheric pressure.</li>\n<li>Thrust varies greatly with external pressure and thus with altitude, even for a non-throttled engine. For example, on Apollo 15, the calculated total liftoff thrust (based on actual measurements) was about 7,830,000&#160;lbf (34,810&#160;kN), which increased to 9,200,000&#160;lbf (40,800&#160;kN) at T+135 seconds, just before center engine cutoff (CECO), at which time the jet was heavily underexpanded.</li>\n<li>Thrust specifications are often given as vacuum thrust (for upper stages) or sea level thrust (for lower stages or <a href="/wiki/Booster_(rocketry)" title="Booster (rocketry)">boosters</a>), sometimes without qualifying which one. This can lead to incorrect comparisons.</li>\n<li>Thrust specifications are often given as average thrust or peak thrust, sometimes without qualifying which one. Even for a non-throttled engine at a fixed altitude, thrust can often vary somewhat over the firing period due to several factors. These include intentional or unintentional mixture ratio changes, slight propellant density changes over the firing period, and variations in turbopump, nozzle and injector performance over the firing period.</li></ul>\n<p>Without knowing the exact measurement technique and mathematical method used to determine thrust for each different rocket, comparisons are often inexact. As the above shows, the specified thrust often differs significantly from actual flight thrust calculated from direct measurements. The thrust stated in various references is often not adequately qualified as to vacuum vs sea level, or peak vs average thrust.\n</p><p>Similarly, payload increases are often achieved in later missions independent of engine thrust. This is by weight reduction or trajectory reshaping.\n</p><p>The result is there is no single absolute figure for engine thrust, stage thrust or vehicle payload. There are specified values and actual flight values, and various ways of measuring and deriving those actual flight values.\n</p><p>The performance of each Saturn V launch was extensively analyzed and a Launch Evaluation Report produced for each mission, including a thrust/time graph for each vehicle stage on each mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insideKSC_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-insideKSC-45">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assembly">Assembly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Assembly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="965" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg/220px-Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg/330px-Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg/440px-Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ap10-KSC-69PC-110.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a> Saturn V during rollout</div></div></div>\n<p>After the construction and ground testing of a stage was completed, it was then shipped to the Kennedy Space Center. The first two stages were so massive that the only way to transport them was by barge. The S-IC, constructed in New Orleans, was transported down the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>.\n</p><p>After rounding <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, it was then transported up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intra-Coastal_Waterway" title="Intra-Coastal Waterway">Intra-Coastal Waterway</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a> (originally called the Vertical Assembly Building). This was essentially the same route which would be used later by NASA to ship Space Shuttle External Tanks. The S-II was constructed in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and thus traveled to Florida via the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. The third stage and Instrument Unit could be carried by the <a href="/wiki/Aero_Spacelines" title="Aero Spacelines">Aero Spacelines</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pregnant_Guppy" title="Pregnant Guppy">Pregnant Guppy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aero_Spacelines_Super_Guppy" title="Aero Spacelines Super Guppy">Super Guppy</a>, but could also have been carried by barge if warranted.\n</p><p>On arrival at the Vertical Assembly Building, each stage was inspected in a horizontal position before being moved to a vertical position. NASA also constructed large spool-shaped structures that could be used in place of stages if a particular stage was late. These spools had the same height and mass and contained the same electrical connections as the actual stages.\n</p><p>NASA <i>stacked</i> or assembled the Saturn V on a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Launcher_Platform" title="Mobile Launcher Platform">Mobile Launcher Platform</a> (MLP), which consisted of a Launch Umbilical Tower (LUT) with nine swing arms (including the crew access arm), a "hammerhead" crane, and a water suppression system which was activated prior to launch. After assembly was completed, the entire stack was moved from the VAB to the launch pad using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crawler_Transporter" title="Crawler Transporter">Crawler Transporter</a> (CT). Built by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marion_Power_Shovel" title="Marion Power Shovel">Marion Power Shovel</a> company (and later used for transporting the smaller and lighter Space Shuttle), the CT ran on four double-tracked treads, each with 57 \'shoes\'. Each shoe weighed 2,000 pounds (910&#160;kg). This transporter was also required to keep the rocket level as it traveled the 3 miles (4.8&#160;km) to the launch site, especially at the 3 percent <a href="/wiki/Grade_(slope)" title="Grade (slope)">grade</a> encountered at the launch pad. The CT also carried the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_Service_Structure" title="Mobile Service Structure">Mobile Service Structure</a> (MSS), which allowed technicians access to the rocket until eight hours before launch, when it was moved to the "halfway" point on the Crawlerway (the junction between the VAB and the two launch pads).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_mission_launch_sequence">Lunar mission launch sequence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lunar mission launch sequence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_Launch2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Apollo_11_Launch2.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_Launch2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Apollo_11_Launch2.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_Launch2.jpg 1.5x,
