data-framerate="29.9699"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Apollo 11 launch, video of engines at 500 fps (camera E-8).ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Apollo_11_launch%2C_video_of_engines_at_500_fps_%28camera_E-8%29.ogv/220px--Apollo_11_launch%2C_video_of_engines_at_500_fps_%28camera_E-8%29.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/Apollo_11_launch%2C_video_of_engines_at_500_fps_%28camera_E-8%29.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_launch,_video_of_engines_at_500_fps_(camera_E-8).ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 11 launch pad filmed at 500 fps.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Saturn V carried all Apollo lunar missions. All Saturn V missions were launched from Launch Complex 39 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Space_Center" title="John F. Kennedy Space Center">John F. Kennedy Space Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. After the rocket cleared the launch tower, flight control transferred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">Mission Control</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>.\n</p><p>An average mission used the rocket for a total of just 20 minutes. Although <a href="/wiki/Apollo_6" title="Apollo 6">Apollo 6</a> experienced three engine failures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lverAS502_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-lverAS502-46">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> one engine shutdown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lverAS508_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-lverAS508-47">[43]</a></sup> the onboard computers were able to compensate by burning the remaining engines longer to achieve parking orbit. None of the Saturn V launches resulted in a payload loss.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="S-IC_sequence">S-IC sequence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: S-IC sequence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Apollo_11_launch.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Apollo_11_launch.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Apollo_11_launch.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_launch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prandtl-Glauert_singularity" title="Prandtl-Glauert singularity">condensation cloud</a> surrounds the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> Saturn V as it works its way through the dense lower atmosphere. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Q" title="Max Q">max Q</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first stage burned for about 2 minutes and 41 seconds, lifting the rocket to an altitude of 42 miles (68&#160;km) and a speed of 6,164 miles per hour (2,756&#160;m/s) and burning 4,700,000 pounds (2,100,000&#160;kg) of propellant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At 8.9 seconds before launch, the first stage ignition sequence started. The center engine ignited first, followed by opposing outboard pairs at 300-millisecond intervals to reduce the structural loads on the rocket. When thrust had been confirmed by the onboard computers, the rocket was "soft-released" in two stages: first, the hold-down arms released the rocket, and second, as the rocket began to accelerate upwards, it was slowed by tapered metal pins pulled through dies for half a second.\n</p><p>Once the rocket had lifted off, it could not safely settle back down onto the pad if the engines failed. The astronauts considered this one of the tensest moments in riding the Saturn V, for <i>if</i> the rocket did fail to lift off after release they had a low chance of survival given the large amounts of propellant. A fully fueled Saturn V exploding on the pad would have released the energy equivalent of two kilotons of TNT. To improve safety, the Saturn Emergency Detection System (EDS) inhibited engine shutdown for the first 30 seconds of flight. (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Instrument_Unit" title="Saturn V Instrument Unit">Saturn V Instrument Unit</a>)\n</p><p>It took about 12 seconds for the rocket to clear the tower. During this time, it <a href="/wiki/Yaw_(rotation)" title="Yaw (rotation)">yawed</a> 1.25 degrees away from the tower to ensure adequate clearance despite adverse winds. (This yaw, although small, can be seen in launch photos taken from the east or west.) At an altitude of 430 feet (130&#160;m) the rocket rolled to the correct flight azimuth and then gradually pitched down until 38 seconds after second stage ignition. This pitch program was set according to the prevailing winds during the launch month.\n</p><p>The four outboard engines also tilted toward the outside so that in the event of a premature outboard engine shutdown the remaining engines would thrust through the rocket\'s <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a>. The Saturn V reached 400 feet per second (120&#160;m/s) at over 1 mile (1,600&#160;m) in altitude. Much of the early portion of the flight was spent gaining altitude, with the required velocity coming later. The Saturn V broke the sound barrier at just over 1 minute at an altitude of between 3 and 4 nautical miles (5.5 to 7.4 kilometers). At this point, shock collars, or condensation clouds, could be seen forming around the bottom of the command module and around the top of the second stage.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_first_stage_separation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 11 S-IC separation</div></div></div>\n<p>At about 80 seconds, the rocket experienced maximum dynamic pressure (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Q" title="Max Q">max <i>Q</i></a>). The <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_pressure" title="Dynamic pressure">dynamic pressure</a> on a rocket varies with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_density" title="Air density">air density</a> and the square of relative velocity. Although velocity continues to increase, air density decreases so quickly with altitude that dynamic pressure falls below max <i>Q</i>.\n</p><p>Acceleration increased during S-IC flight for three reasons. One, increased acceleration increased the propellant pressure at the engines, increasing the flow rate somewhat. This was the least important factor, though this feedback effect often led to an undesirable thrust oscillation called <a href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillation" title="Pogo oscillation">pogo</a>. Two, as it climbed into thinner air F-1 engine efficiency increased significantly, a property of all rockets. The combined thrust of five engines on the pad was about 7.5 million pounds, reaching nearly 9 million pounds at altitude. But the biggest contribution by far was the rocket\'s rapidly decreasing mass.\n</p><p>The propellant in just the S-IC made up about three-quarters of Saturn V\'s entire launch mass, and it was furiously consumed at over 13 metric tonnes per second. Newton\'s second law states that force is equal to mass multiplied by acceleration, or equivalently that acceleration is equal to force divided by mass, so as the mass decreased (and the force increased somewhat), acceleration rose. Including gravity, launch acceleration was only 1&#188;&#160;<a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force"><i>g</i></a>, i.e., the astronauts felt 1&#188;&#160;g while the rocket accelerated vertically at &#188;&#160;g. As the rocket rapidly lost mass, total acceleration including gravity increased to nearly 4&#160;g at T+135 seconds. At this point, the inboard (center) engine was shut down to prevent acceleration from increasing beyond 4&#160;g.\n</p><p>When oxidizer or fuel depletion was sensed in the suction assemblies, the remaining four outboard engines were shut down. First stage separation occurred a little less than one second after this to allow for F-1 thrust tail-off. Eight small solid fuel separation motors backed the S-IC from the rest of the vehicle at an altitude of about 36 nautical miles (67&#160;km). The first stage continued ballistically to an altitude of about 59 nautical miles (109&#160;km) and then fell in the Atlantic Ocean about 300 nautical miles (560&#160;km) downrange.\n</p><p>The engine shutdown procedure was changed for the launch of <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> to avoid damage to the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">Apollo Telescope Mount</a>. Rather than shutting down all four outboard engines at once, they were shut down two at a time with a delay to reduce peak acceleration further.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="S-II_sequence">S-II sequence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: S-II sequence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg/220px-Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg/330px-Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg/440px-Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ap6-68-HC-191.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Still from film footage of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_6" title="Apollo 6">Apollo 6</a>\'s interstage falling away</div></div></div>\n<p>After S-IC separation, the S-II second stage burned for 6 minutes and propelled the craft to 109 miles (175&#160;km) and 15,647&#160;mph (6,995&#160;m/s), close to <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital velocity</a>.\n</p><p>For the first two unmanned launches, eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel#Solid_propellants" title="Rocket fuel">solid-fuel</a> <a href="/wiki/Ullage_motor" title="Ullage motor">ullage motors</a> ignited for four seconds to give positive acceleration to the S-II stage, followed by start of the five J-2 engines. For the first seven manned Apollo missions only four ullage motors were used on the S-II, and they were eliminated completely for the final four launches. About 30 seconds after first stage separation, the interstage ring dropped from the second stage. This was done with an inertially fixed attitude so that the interstage, only 1 meter from the outboard J-2 engines, would fall cleanly without hitting them, as the interstage could have potentially damaged two of the J-2 engines if it was attached to the S-IC. Shortly after interstage separation the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Escape_System" title="Launch Escape System">Launch Escape System</a> was also jettisoned. See <a href="/wiki/Apollo_abort_modes" title="Apollo abort modes">Apollo abort modes</a> for more information about the various abort modes that could have been used during a launch.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo6Interstage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Apollo6Interstage.jpg/220px-Apollo6Interstage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Apollo6Interstage.jpg/330px-Apollo6Interstage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Apollo6Interstage.jpg/440px-Apollo6Interstage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo6Interstage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 6 interstage falling away. The engine exhaust from the S-II stage glows as it impacts the interstage.</div></div></div>\n<p>About 38 seconds after the second stage ignition the Saturn V switched from a preprogrammed trajectory to a "closed loop" or Iterative Guidance Mode. The Instrument Unit now computed in real time the most fuel-efficient trajectory toward its target orbit. If the Instrument Unit failed, the crew could switch control of the Saturn to the Command Module\'s computer, take manual control, or abort the flight.\n</p><p>About 90 seconds before the second stage cutoff, the center engine shut down to reduce longitudinal <a href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillation" title="Pogo oscillation">pogo oscillations</a>. At around this time, the LOX flow rate decreased, changing the mix ratio of the two propellants, ensuring that there would be as little propellant as possible left in the tanks at the end of second stage flight. This was done at a predetermined <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>.\n</p><p>Five level sensors in the bottom of each S-II propellant tank were armed during S-II flight, allowing any two to trigger S-II cutoff and staging when they were uncovered. One second after the second stage cut off it separated and several seconds later the third stage ignited. Solid fuel retro-rockets mounted on the interstage at the top of the S-II fired to back it away from the S-IVB. The <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> impacted about 2,300 nautical miles (4,200&#160;km) from the launch site.\n</p><p>On the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> mission, the inboard engine suffered from major pogo oscillation, resulting in an early automatic cutoff. To ensure sufficient velocity was reached, the remaining four engines were kept active for longer than planned. A pogo suppressor was fitted to later Apollo missions to avoid this, though the early engine 5 cutoff remained to reduce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G-forces" title="G-forces">g-forces</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="S-IVB_sequence">S-IVB sequence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: S-IVB sequence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Unlike the two-plane separation of the S-IC and S-II, the S-II and S-IVB stages separated with a single step. Although it was constructed as part of the third stage, the interstage remained attached to the second stage.\n</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>, a typical lunar mission, the third stage burned for about 2.5 minutes until first cutoff at 11 minutes 40 seconds. At this point it was 1,430 nautical miles (2,650&#160;km)&#160; downrange and in a parking orbit at an altitude of 103.2 nautical miles (191.1&#160;km)&#160; and velocity of 17,432&#160;mph (7,793&#160;m/s). The third stage remained attached to the spacecraft while it <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbited</a> the Earth one and a half times while astronauts and mission controllers prepared for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translunar_injection" title="Translunar injection">translunar injection</a> (TLI).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1664" data-file-width="1968" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg/220px-AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg/330px-AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg/440px-AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AS17-148-22714_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo 17 <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> rocket stage, shortly after transposition and docking with the Lunar Module</div></div></div>\n<p>This parking orbit was quite low by Earth orbit standards, and it would have been short-lived due to aerodynamic drag. This was not a problem on a lunar mission because of the short stay in the parking orbit. The S-IVB also continued to thrust at a low level by venting gaseous hydrogen, to keep propellants settled in their tanks and prevent gaseous cavities from forming in propellant feed lines. This venting also maintained safe pressures as liquid hydrogen boiled off in the fuel tank. This venting thrust easily exceeded aerodynamic drag.\n</p><p>For the final three Apollo flights, the temporary parking orbit was even lower (approximately 93 nautical miles (172&#160;km)), to increase payload for these missions. The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_9" title="Apollo 9">Apollo 9</a> Earth orbit mission was launched into the nominal orbit consistent with Apollo 11, but the spacecraft were able to use their own engines to raise the perigee high enough to sustain the 10-day mission. The <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> was launched into a quite different orbit, with a 234-nautical-mile (434&#160;km) perigee which sustained it for six years, and also a higher inclination to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> (50 degrees versus 32.5 degrees for Apollo).\n</p><p>On Apollo 11, TLI came at 2 hours and 44 minutes after launch. The S-IVB burned for almost six minutes giving the spacecraft a velocity close to the Earth\'s escape velocity of 25,053&#160;mph (11,200&#160;m/s). This gave an energy-efficient transfer to lunar orbit, with the Moon helping to capture the spacecraft with a minimum of CSM fuel consumption.\n</p><p>About 40 minutes after TLI the Apollo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_Service_Module" title="Command Service Module">Command Service Module</a> (CSM) separated from the third stage, turned 180 degrees and docked with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Module" title="Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> (LM) that rode below the CSM during launch. The CSM and LM separated from the spent third stage 50 minutes later. This process is known as <a href="/wiki/Transposition,_docking,_and_extraction" title="Transposition, docking, and extraction">Transposition, docking, and extraction</a>.\n</p><p>If it were to remain on the same trajectory as the spacecraft, the S-IVB could have presented a collision hazard so its remaining propellants were vented and the auxiliary propulsion system fired to move it away. For lunar missions before <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a>, the S-IVB was directed toward the Moon\'s trailing edge in its orbit so that the moon would <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">slingshot</a> it beyond earth escape velocity and into solar orbit. From Apollo 13 onwards, controllers directed the S-IVB to hit the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impact_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-impact-49">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">Seismometers</a> left behind by previous missions detected the impacts, and the information helped map the interior structure of the Moon.\n</p><p>On September 3, 2002, <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_Yeung" title="Bill Yeung">Bill Yeung</a> discovered a suspected <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>, which was given the discovery designation <a href="/wiki/J002E3" title="J002E3">J002E3</a>. It appeared to be in orbit around the Earth, and was soon discovered from spectral analysis to be covered in white <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titanium dioxide</a>, which was a major constituent of the paint used on the Saturn V. Calculation of orbital parameters led to tentative identification as being the Apollo 12 S-IVB stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-50">[46]</a></sup> Mission controllers had planned to send Apollo 12\'s S-IVB into solar orbit after separating from the Apollo spacecraft, but it is believed the burn lasted too long, and hence did not send it close enough to the Moon, remaining in a barely stable orbit around the Earth and Moon. In 1971, through a series of gravitational perturbations, it is believed to have entered in a solar orbit and then returned into weakly captured Earth orbit 31 years later. It left Earth orbit again in June 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jorgensen_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jorgensen-51">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Skylab">Skylab</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Skylab">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="2205" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg/220px-Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg/330px-Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg/440px-Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skylab-73-HC-440HR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The last Saturn V launch carried the Skylab space station to low Earth orbit in place of the third stage</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1965, the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">Apollo Applications Program</a> (AAP) was created to look into science missions that could be performed using Apollo hardware. Much of the planning centered on the idea of a space station. <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a>\'s earlier (1964) plans employed a "<a href="/wiki/Wet_workshop" title="Wet workshop">wet workshop</a>" concept, with a spent <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> Saturn V second stage being launched into orbit and outfitted in space. The next year AAP studied a smaller station using an <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_1B" title="Saturn 1B">Saturn 1B</a> second stage. By 1969, Apollo funding cuts eliminated the possibility of procuring more Apollo hardware, and in fact forced the cancellation of some later Moon landing flights. This freed up at least one Saturn V, allowing the wet workshop to be replaced with the "dry workshop" concept: the station (now known as <i>Skylab</i>) would be built on the ground from a surplus Saturn IB second stage and launched atop the first two live stages of a Saturn V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Saturn_V_F-1_Engine_(Skylab)_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Saturn_V_F-1_Engine_(Skylab)-52">[48]</a></sup> A backup station, constructed from a Saturn V third stage, was built and is now on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>.\n</p><p>Skylab was the only launch not directly related to the Apollo lunar landing program. The only significant changes to the Saturn V from the Apollo configurations involved some modification to the <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a> to act as the terminal stage for inserting the Skylab payload into Earth orbit, and to vent excess propellant after engine cutoff so the spent stage would not rupture in orbit. The S-II remained in orbit for almost two years, and made an uncontrolled re-entry on January 11, 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three crews lived aboard Skylab from May 25, 1973 to February 8, 1974, with Skylab remaining in orbit until July 11, 1979.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_post-Apollo_developments">Proposed post-Apollo developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Proposed post-Apollo developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2070" decoding="async" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg/220px-Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg/330px-Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg/440px-Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-Shuttle_model_at_Udvar-Hazy_Center.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Saturn-Shuttle" title="Saturn-Shuttle">Saturn-Shuttle</a> concept</div></div></div>\n<p>After Apollo, the Saturn V was planned to be the prime launch vehicle for <a href="/wiki/Prospector_(spacecraft)" title="Prospector (spacecraft)">Prospector</a> intended to land a 330-kilogram (730&#160;lb) robotic rover on the Moon, similar to the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_programme" title="Lunokhod programme">Lunokhod</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program_(Mars)" title="Voyager program (Mars)"><i>Voyager</i> Mars probes</a>, as well a scaled-up version of the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> interplanetary probes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cortright_Oral_History_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cortright_Oral_History-55">[51]</a></sup> It was also to have been the launch vehicle for the nuclear rocket stage RIFT test program and the later <a href="/wiki/NERVA" title="NERVA">NERVA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup> All of these planned uses of the Saturn V were cancelled, with cost being a major factor. <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cortright" title="Edgar Cortright">Edgar Cortright</a>, who had been director of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Langley" title="NASA Langley">NASA Langley</a>, stated decades later that "JPL never liked the big approach. They always argued against it. I probably was the leading proponent in using the Saturn V, and I lost. Probably very wise that I lost."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cortright_Oral_History_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cortright_Oral_History-55">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The canceled second production run of Saturn Vs would very likely have used the <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1#F-1A_after_Apollo" title="Rocketdyne F-1">F-1A</a> engine in its first stage, providing a substantial performance boost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaturnGenealogy_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SaturnGenealogy-57">[53]</a></sup> Other likely changes would have been the removal of the fins (which turned out to provide little benefit when compared to their weight); a stretched S-IC first stage to support the more powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1As</a>; and uprated J-2s or an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M-1_(rocket_engine)" title="M-1 (rocket engine)">M-1</a> for the upper stages.\n</p><p>A number of alternate Saturn vehicles were proposed based on the Saturn V, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_INT-20" title="Saturn INT-20">Saturn INT-20</a> with an <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> stage and interstage mounted directly onto an <a href="/wiki/S-IC" title="S-IC">S-IC</a> stage, through to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V-23(L)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saturn V-23(L) (page does not exist)">Saturn V-23(L)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA-23L_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-23L-58">[54]</a></sup> which would not only have five F-1 engines in the first stage, but also four strap-on <a href="/wiki/Booster_(rocketry)" title="Booster (rocketry)">boosters</a> with two F-1 engines each: giving a total of thirteen F-1 engines firing at launch.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> was initially conceived as a cargo transport to be used in concert with the Saturn V, even to the point that a <a href="/wiki/Saturn-Shuttle" title="Saturn-Shuttle">Saturn-Shuttle</a> was proposed, using the winged shuttle orbiter and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_tank" title="External tank">external tank</a>, but with the tank mounted on a modified, fly-back version of the S-IC. The first S-IC stage would be used to power the Shuttle during the first two minutes of flight, after which the S-IC would be jettisoned (which would then fly back to KSC for refurbishment) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engines</a> would then fire and place the orbiter into orbit. The Shuttle would handle <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> logistics, while Saturn V would launch components.\n</p><p>Lack of a second Saturn V production run killed this plan and left the United States without a <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_orbital_launch_systems" title="Comparison of orbital launch systems">heavy-lift launch vehicle</a>. Some in the U.S. space community came to lament this situation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> as continued production would have allowed the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, using a Skylab or <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> configuration with both U.S. and Russian docking ports, to have been lifted with just a handful of launches. The <a href="/wiki/Saturn-Shuttle" title="Saturn-Shuttle">Saturn-Shuttle</a> concept also would have eliminated the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Boosters</a> that ultimately precipitated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_Disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger Disaster"><i>Challenger</i> accident</a> in 1986.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost">Cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>From 1964 until 1973, $6.417 billion (equivalent to $33&#160;billion in 2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-60">[56]</a></sup> in total was appropriated for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%26D" title="R&amp;D">R&amp;D</a> and flights of the Saturn V, with the maximum being in 1966 with $1.2 billion (equivalent to $6.95&#160;billion in 2016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-budget_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-budget-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-60">[56]</a></sup> That same year, NASA received its biggest budget of $4.5 billion, about 0.5 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States" title="Economy of the United States">gross domestic product (GDP) of the United States</a> at that time.\n</p><p>One of the main reasons for the cancellation of the last three Apollo flights was the cost. In the time frame from 1969 to 1971 the cost of launching a Saturn V Apollo mission was $185,000,000&#8211;$189,000,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-budget_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-budget-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP4221Ch6_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-SP4221Ch6-2">[2]</a></sup> of which $110 million was for the production of the vehicle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.nasa.gov_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-history.nasa.gov-3">[3]</a></sup> (equivalent to $566&#160;million in 2016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-60">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_V_vehicles_and_launches">Saturn V vehicles and launches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Saturn V vehicles and launches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_launches.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Saturn_V_launches.jpg/400px-Saturn_V_launches.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Saturn_V_launches.jpg/600px-Saturn_V_launches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Saturn_V_launches.jpg/800px-Saturn_V_launches.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_launches.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>All Saturn V launches, 1967&#8211;1973</div></div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="background:#efefef;">Serial<br/>number<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serialnote_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-serialnote-8">[note 2]</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th to check precise fits and test facilities operation on Pad 39A before a flight model was ready. First stage scrapped, second stage converted to <a href="/wiki/S-II" title="S-II">S-II</a>-F/D, third stage on display at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-displays_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-displays-61">[57]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Saturn_V_Dynamic_Test_Vehicle" title="Saturn V Dynamic Test Vehicle">SA-500D</a>\n</th>\n<td colspan="3" style="text-align:center;">Dynamic testing\n</td>\n<td>Used to evaluate the vehicle\'s response to vibrations. complete success.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-502\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_6" title="Apollo 6">Apollo 6</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">April 4, 1968\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Second unmanned test flight; J-2 engine problems caused early shutdown of two engines in second stage, and prevented third stage restart.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-503\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">December 21, 1968\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>First manned flight; first translunar injection of Apollo Command/Service Module\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-504\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_9" title="Apollo 9">Apollo 9</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">March 3, 1969\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Manned low Earth orbit test of complete Apollo spacecraft with LM\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-505\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">May 18, 1969\n</td>\n<td align="center">39B\n</td>\n<td>Second manned translunar injection of complete Apollo spacecraft with LM\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-506\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">July 16, 1969\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>First manned lunar landing, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_of_Tranquility" title="Sea of Tranquility">Sea of Tranquility</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-507\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">November 14, 1969\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Vehicle was struck twice by lightning shortly after liftoff, no serious damage. Precision manned lunar landing, near <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_3" title="Surveyor 3">Surveyor 3</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_of_Storms" title="Ocean of Storms">Ocean of Storms</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-508\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">April 11, 1970\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pogo_oscillations" title="Pogo oscillations">pogo oscillations</a> in second stage caused early center engine shutdown; guidance compensated by burning remaining engines longer. Third manned lunar landing mission was aborted by Service Module failure.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-509\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">January 31, 1971\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Third manned lunar landing, near <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_(crater)" title="Fra Mauro (crater)">Fra Mauro</a>, Apollo 13\'s intended landing site.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-510\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">July 26, 1971\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Fourth manned lunar landing, at <a href="/wiki/Hadley%E2%80%93Apennine" title="Hadley&#8211;Apennine">Hadley&#8211;Apennine</a>. First extended Apollo mission, carrying lunar orbital Scientific Instrument Module and <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">Lunar Roving Vehicle</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-511\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">Apollo 16</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">April 16, 1972\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Fifth manned lunar landing, at <a href="/wiki/Descartes_Highlands" title="Descartes Highlands">Descartes Highlands</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-512\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center">December 7, 1972\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Only night launch. Sixth and final manned lunar landing, at <a href="/wiki/Taurus%E2%80%93Littrow" title="Taurus&#8211;Littrow">Taurus&#8211;Littrow</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-513\n</th>\n<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> 1\n</td>\n<td align="center">May 14, 1973\n</td>\n<td align="center">39A\n</td>\n<td>Unmanned launch of the Skylab orbital workshop, which replaced the third stage, S-IVB-513, on display at <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-displays_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-displays-61">[57]</a></sup> Originally designated for <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Apollo_missions" title="Canceled Apollo missions">canceled Apollo 18</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-514\n</th>\n<td colspan="3" style="text-align:center;">Unused\n</td>\n<td>Originally designated for canceled Apollo 19; never used. First stage (S-IC-14) on display at <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>, second and third stage (S-II-14, S-IV-14) on display at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-displays_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-displays-61">[57]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th align="center">SA-515\n</th>\n<td colspan="3" style="text-align:center;">Unused\n</td>\n<td>Originally designated for Apollo 20, later as a backup Skylab launch vehicle; never used. The first stage was on display at <a href="/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility" title="Michoud Assembly Facility">Michoud Assembly Facility</a>, until June 2016 then was moved to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INFINITY_Science_Center" title="INFINITY Science Center">INFINITY Science Center</a> in Mississippi. The second stage (S-II-15) is on display at Johnson Space Center. The third stage was converted to a backup Skylab orbital workshop and is on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-displays_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-displays-61">[57]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_successors">Proposed successors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Proposed successors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M-1_(rocket_engine)" title="M-1 (rocket engine)">M-1 (rocket engine)</a>, <a href="/wiki/NERVA" title="NERVA">NERVA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion (spacecraft)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_Dragon_(rocket)" title="Sea Dragon (rocket)">Sea Dragon (rocket)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maximum_payload.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Maximum_payload.PNG/350px-Maximum_payload.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Maximum_payload.PNG/525px-Maximum_payload.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/50/Maximum_payload.PNG/700px-Maximum_payload.PNG 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maximum_payload.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of maximum payload to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> (LEO) (Left to right). <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> payload includes 7 crew and cargo. <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares I</a> payload includes 4 crew and inherent craft. Saturn V payload includes 3 crew, inherent craft and cargo. <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a> payload includes only cargo and inherent craft. The Saturn V was capable of lifting approximately 140 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> of payload to LEO. The Ares V was being designed to lift 188 metric tons to LEO.</div></div></div>\n<p>U.S. proposals for a rocket larger than the Saturn V from the late 1950s through the early 1980s were generally called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nova_rocket" title="Nova rocket">Nova</a>. Over thirty different large rocket proposals carried the Nova name, but none was developed.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> and others also had plans for a rocket that would have featured eight F-1 engines in its first stage, like the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_C-8" title="Saturn C-8">Saturn C-8</a>, allowing a <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">direct ascent</a> flight to the Moon. Other plans for the Saturn V called for using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_rocket" title="Centaur rocket">Centaur</a> as an upper stage or <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V_ELV" title="Saturn V ELV">adding strap-on boosters</a>. These enhancements would have enabled the launch of large robotic spacecraft to the outer <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> or send astronauts to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. Other Saturn-V derivatives analyzed included the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_MLV" title="Saturn MLV">Saturn MLV</a> family of "Modified Launch Vehicles", which would have almost doubled the payload lift capability of the standard Saturn V and were intended for use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_manned_Mars_mission_plans_in_the_20th_century" title="List of manned Mars mission plans in the 20th century">a proposed mission to Mars by 1980</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> studied another Saturn-V derivative, the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_C-5N" title="Saturn C-5N">Saturn C-5N</a>, which included a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_thermal_rocket" title="Nuclear thermal rocket">nuclear thermal rocket</a> engine for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_staging" title="Rocket staging">third stage</a> of the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> The Saturn C-5N would carry a considerably greater payload to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_space" title="Interplanetary space">interplanetary</a> destinations. Work on the nuclear engines, along with all <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V_ELV" title="Saturn V ELV">Saturn V ELVs</a>, was ended in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ares-I-X_2009-5938_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="860" data-file-width="583" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Ares-I-X_2009-5938_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ares-I-X_2009-5938_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2009 test flight of the prototype <a href="/wiki/Ares_I-X" title="Ares I-X">Ares I-X</a> launch vehicle, a component of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constellation_Program" title="Constellation Program">Constellation Program</a> that was cancelled in 2010</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2006, as part of the proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constellation_Program" title="Constellation Program">Constellation Program</a>, NASA unveiled plans to construct two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle_Derived_Launch_Vehicle" title="Shuttle Derived Launch Vehicle">Shuttle Derived Launch Vehicles</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ares_I" title="Ares I">Ares I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">Ares V</a>, which would use some existing Space Shuttle and Saturn V hardware and infrastructure. The two rockets were intended to increase safety by <a href="/wiki/Specialization_(functional)" title="Specialization (functional)">specializing</a> each vehicle for different tasks, Ares I for crew launches and Ares V for cargo launches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> The original design of the heavy-lift Ares V, named in homage to the Saturn V, was 360&#160;ft (110&#160;m) in height and featured a core stage based on the Space Shuttle External Tank, with a diameter of 28&#160;ft (8.4&#160;m). It was to be powered by five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Main_Engine" title="Space Shuttle Main Engine">Space Shuttle Main Engines</a> (SSMEs) and two five-segment <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Boosters</a> (SRBs). As the design evolved, the SSMEs were replaced with five <a href="/wiki/RS-68" title="RS-68">RS-68</a> engines, the same engines used on the <a href="/wiki/Delta_IV" title="Delta IV">Delta IV</a>. The switch from the SSME to the RS-68 was intended to reduce cost, the RS-68 being cheaper, simpler to manufacture, and more powerful than the SSME, though the lower <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">efficiency</a> of the RS-68 required an increase in core stage diameter to 33&#160;ft (10&#160;m), the same diameter as the Saturn V\'s S-IC and S-II stages.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png/400px-Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png/600px-Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png/800px-Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V-Shuttle-Ares_I-Ares_V-Ares_IV-SLS_Block_I_comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of Saturn V, Shuttle, Ares I, Ares V, Ares IV, and SLS Block I</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2008, NASA again redesigned the Ares V, lengthening the core stage, adding a sixth RS-68 engine, and increasing the SRBs to 5.5 segments each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ares_V_Overview_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ares_V_Overview-67">[63]</a></sup> This vehicle would have been 381&#160;ft (116&#160;m) tall and would have produced a total thrust of approximately 8,900,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a> (40&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">MN</a>) at liftoff, more than the Saturn V or the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Energia" title="Energia">Energia</a>, but less than the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-1_(rocket)" title="N-1 (rocket)">N-1</a>. Projected to place approximately 180 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> into orbit, the Ares V would have surpassed the Saturn V in payload capability. An upper stage, the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Departure_Stage" title="Earth Departure Stage">Earth Departure Stage</a>, would have utilized a more advanced version of the J-2 engine, the <a href="/wiki/J-2X" title="J-2X">J-2X</a>. Ares V would have placed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Surface_Access_Module" title="Lunar Surface Access Module">Altair</a> lunar landing vehicle into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft)" title="Orion (spacecraft)">Orion</a> crew vehicle launched on Ares I would have docked with Altair, and the Earth Departure Stage would then send the combined stack to the Moon.\n</p><p>After the cancellation of the Constellation Program &#8211; and hence Ares I and Ares V &#8211; NASA announced the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a> (SLS) heavy-lift launch vehicle for deep-space exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Announces_Design_for_New_Deep_Space_Exploration_System_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Announces_Design_for_New_Deep_Space_Exploration_System-68">[64]</a></sup> The SLS, similar to the original Ares V concept, will be powered by four SSMEs and two five-segment SRBs. Its Block I configuration will lift approximately 70 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> to <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>. Block IB will add a second stage, the <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage" title="Exploration Upper Stage">Exploration Upper Stage</a>, powered by four <a href="/wiki/RL10" title="RL10">RL10</a> engines, to increase payload to LEO and deep space. An eventual Block II variant will upgrade to advanced boosters, increasing LEO payload to at least 130 metric tons.\n</p><p>One proposal for advanced boosters would use a derivative of the Saturn V\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-1_(rocket_engine)" title="F-1 (rocket engine)">F-1</a>, the F-1B, and increase SLS payload to around 150 metric tons to LEO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup> The F-1B is to have better <a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">specific impulse</a> and be cheaper than the F-1, with a simplified combustion chamber and fewer engine parts, while producing 1,800,000&#160;lbf (8.0&#160;MN) of thrust at sea level, an increase over the approximate 1,550,000&#160;lbf (6.9&#160;MN) achieved by the mature <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a> F-1 engine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NASA SLS deputy project manager Jody Singer of the Marshall Space Flight Center in Huntsville, in 2012 stated that the vehicle will have a launch cost of approximately $500 million per launch, with a relatively minor dependence of costs on launch capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_V_displays">Saturn V displays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Saturn V displays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li>Two at the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center</a> in Huntsville:</li></ul>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SA-500D" title="SA-500D">SA-500D</a> is on horizontal display made up of S-IC-D, S-II-F/D and S-IVB-D. These were all test stages not meant for flight. This vehicle was displayed outdoors from 1969 to 2007, was restored, and is now displayed in the Davidson Center for Space Exploration.</li>\n<li>Vertical display (replica) built in 1999 located in an adjacent area.</li></ul></dd></dl>\n<ul><li>One at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a> made up of first stage from SA-514, the second stage from SA-515 and the third stage from SA-513 (replaced for flight by the Skylab workshop). With stages arriving between 1977 and 1979, this was displayed in the open until its 2005 restoration when a structure was built around it for protection. This is the only display Saturn consisting entirely of stages intended to be launched.</li>\n<li>One at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Visitor_Complex" title="Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex">Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex</a>, made up of S-IC-T (test stage) and the second and third stages from SA-514.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> It was displayed outdoors for decades, then in 1996 was enclosed for protection from the elements in the Apollo/Saturn V Center.</li>\n<li>The S-IC stage from SA-515 is on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INFINITY_Science_Center" title="INFINITY Science Center">Infinity Science Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>.</li>\n<li>The S-IVB stage from SA-515 was converted for use as a backup for <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a>, and is on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>.</li></ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:35px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/120px-Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/180px-Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/240px-Saturn_V_rocket_booster_National_Air_and_Space_Museum_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:51px auto;"><a class="image" Rocket Center\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:35px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_building_Johnson_Space_Center.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1361" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Saturn_V_building_Johnson_Space_Center.jpg/120px-Saturn_V_building_Johnson_Space_Center.jpg" T+40s.</div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_V_in_fiction">Saturn V in fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saturn_V&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Saturn V in fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Film footage of the Saturn V appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series" title="Star Trek: The Original Series">Star Trek</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">TV</a> episode "<a href="/wiki/Assignment:_Earth" title="Assignment: Earth">Assignment: Earth</a>", originally broadcast on March 29, 1968. Saturn V and the Apollo program are not mentioned by name, but the rocket was used as a stand-in for the launching of a fictional "<a href="/wiki/Space_weapon#Space-to-Earth_weapons" title="Space weapon">orbital nuclear warhead platform</a> by the United States, countering a similar launch by other powers."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Pre-launch footage comes from the unflown <a href="/wiki/SA-500F" title="SA-500F">SA-500F</a> Facilities Integration Vehicle (the only Saturn V with "USA" markings on the third stage) and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_6" title="Apollo 6">Apollo 6</a> (the only Saturn V launched with a white service module), while launch footage comes from <a href="/wiki/Apollo_4" title="Apollo 4">Apollo 4</a> (as the episode aired six days prior to the launch of Apollo 6, the second Saturn V launch.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Footage of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a> launch was used in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_18_(film)" title="Apollo 18 (film)">Apollo 18</a></i> to represent the fictional mission.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Johnson Space Center">Manned Spacecraft Center</a> started using the format "AS-5xx" (for Apollo-Saturn).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-infonote1-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a
